Misapplying 'Creator of Everything' to Attributes Only
Exposes the error of restricting divine creation to attributes only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the error of limiting the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' to God's attributes while excluding human actions, and how this contradicts the general meaning.

Core Idea
Divine creation is all-encompassing—it includes both attributes and actions.

This lesson examines a theological mistake where some scholars restricted 'Creator of everything' to Allah's attributes (like speech) but refused to apply it to human actions. We'll see the inconsistency in their reasoning and why it must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the error
Read the Arabic passage about how some diverted the meaning of the verse. Notice that they applied it to attributes but not to actions.
The verse is general—why would they exclude actions?
2
Analyze the logical inconsistency
Ask yourself: If 'everything' truly means everything, then human actions must also be created by Allah. Why would some scholars exempt them?
This is a key point of the refutation.
3
Connect to the broader lesson
Reflect on how this error leads to denying divine sovereignty over human deeds, which is a stance the author firmly opposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث صرفوا قوله تعالى: ﴿ اللهُ خَالِقُ كُلّ شَيْء ﴾ [الزمر: ٦٢] إلى صفة الله حتى قالوا: إن كلامه مخلوق، ولم يصرفوه إلى صفات الخلق حتى قالوا: إن أفعال العباد غير مخلوقة له، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ رَمَى ﴾ [الأنفال: ١٧]، فمعناه: ما رميت خلقًا إذ رميت كسبًا، ولكن الله رمى بخلق كسب الرمي في المصطفى صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم. قال الإمام الأعظم في كتابه "الوصية": نقر بأن العبد مع جميع أعماله وإقراره ومعرفته مخلوق، فلما كان الفاعل مخلوقًا فأفعاله أولى أن تكون مخلوقة. انتهى.
Whereby they diverted His statement, the Exalted: { Allah is the Creator of everything } [Az-Zumar: 62] to the attribute of Allah until they said: His speech is created, and they did not divert it to the attributes of creation until they said: The actions of servants are not created for Him. As for His statement, the Exalted: { And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw } [Al-Anfal: 17], its meaning is: You did not throw (as) creation when you threw (as) acquisition, but Allah threw by creating the acquisition of throwing upon the Chosen One, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. The Greatest Imam said in his book 'Al-Wasiyya': We affirm that the servant, along with all his deeds, his confession, and his knowledge, is created. Since the agent is created, his actions are more deserving of being created. End.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage refutes a theological error. It explains that some scholars misapplied the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' only to God's attribute of speech (claiming the Quran is created) but refused to extend it to human actions. The author then clarifies the correct interpretation of the verse 'And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' (Al-Anfal: 17): the Prophet's throwing was an acquired action (kasb), not a creative act; Allah is the one who created the effect. The author then quotes Imam Abu Hanifa from his book 'Al-Wasiyya', who affirms that the servant with all his deeds, confession, and knowledge is created. Since the servant is created, his actions are even more fitting to be created. The argument is that if the agent is contingent and created, then his actions—which depend on him—must also be created.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
صفات الله (attributes of Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that 'Creator of everything' applies only to Allah's attributes, not to human actions.
Correction: The verse is general and includes both attributes and actions; human actions are also created by Allah.

Checkpoint
What error does the author identify regarding the interpretation of 'Allah is the Creator of everything'?
A) Applying it only to God's essence
B) Applying it only to God's attributes, not human actions
C) Denying that God creates anything
D) Claiming the verse is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' is all-encompassing and must include human actions, contrary to those who restrict it to attributes only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you unconsciously act as if your actions are outside Allah's creation—your career, relationships, or private choices? What would change if you truly believed every movement is created by Him?
?
When you recite 'Allah is the Creator of everything,' do you make an exception for your own faults or achievements? Write down one specific action today that you must now attribute to Allah's creation rather than your independent will.
?
If your most difficult sin was also created by Allah, how does that reshape your understanding of His mercy and your responsibility? Can you still repent wholeheartedly while acknowledging His creative power over that very act?

The Kasb Distinction and Imam Abu Hanifa's Affirmation
Clarifies the kasb-khalq distinction and affirms creation of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of 'And you did not throw' using the kasb-khalq distinction, and Imam Abu Hanifa's decisive statement that the servant and his actions are created.

Core Idea
Human actions are created by Allah; we only acquire them through kasb.

This lesson clarifies the verse about the Prophet's throwing, showing that the Prophet's action was an acquisition (kasb) while Allah is the true creator of the effect. Then Imam Abu Hanifa affirms that the servant, along with all his deeds and knowledge, is created by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the interpretation of the verse
Read the Arabic verse and its explanation. Notice the distinction between 'throw as creation' and 'throw as acquisition'.
Kasb means acquisition; khalq means creation.
2
Understand the kasb-khalq distinction
The author explains that when the Prophet threw, he performed the action as an acquisition, but Allah created the throwing (the effect). This preserves divine sovereignty while affirming human responsibility.
3
Study Imam Abu Hanifa's statement
Read the quote from Al-Wasiyya: the servant and all his actions are created. Since the servant is created, his actions are even more deserving of being created.
This is a logical argument from cause to effect.

Misconception: Some think that human actions are not created by Allah because the verse says 'you did not throw' meaning the Prophet created the action.
Correction: The verse means the Prophet did not create the throwing; Allah created it. The Prophet's action was an acquisition (kasb), not a creation.

?

?

Divine Attributes

All Actions Are by God's Will, Obedience by His Command
The author enumerates divine attributes related to human actions and distinguishes obedience from disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how all human actions relate to God's will, knowledge, decree, and predestination, and why obedience and disobedience differ in God's love and command.

Core Idea
God wills all actions, but loves only obedience and commands only good.

In Islamic theology, it is essential to distinguish between God's universal will, which encompasses everything, and His specific love and command, which are directed only to good. This passage from the Sharh of Fiqh al-Akbar clarifies that while all actions — even sins — occur by God's will and decree, obedience is also by His command and pleasure, whereas disobedience is not. Understanding this nuance preserves both divine sovereignty and moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translations
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the repeated attribution of actions to God's will, knowledge, decree, and predestination.
The passage uses a repetitive structure for emphasis.
2
Identify the two categories
The author distinguishes between actions of obedience and actions of disobedience. For each, identify which divine attributes are affirmed and which are denied.
Obedience is linked to love and command; disobedience is not.
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
For each claim, the author cites Quranic verses. List the verses used for obedience and for disobedience, and see how they support the distinctions.
Note the verse about God not commanding immorality.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it protects God from being the author of evil while affirming His absolute sovereignty. The gnostic saying 'choose not to choose' illustrates the ideal of submission.
This balances qadar and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي شرح الفقه الأكبر كلها بمشيئته وعلمه وقضائه وقدره.
صور الطاعات محبوبة لله والمعاصي مقدورة غير محبوبة والطاعات كلها ما كانت واجبةً بأمر الله تعالى وبمحبّته وبرضائه وعلمه ومشيئته وقضائه وتقديره، والمعاصي كلها بعلمه وقضائه وتقديره ومشيئته
قلت: وذلك لوقوع فعل العبد على وفق اختياره من غير تأثير لقدرته المقارنة له، ويؤيده قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ مَا كَانَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ سُبْحَنَ اللَّهِ وَتَعَلَى عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ ﴾ [القصص: ٦٨] ، ولذا قال بعض العارفين لا تختر ، فإن كنت لابد أن تختار فاختر أن لا تختار. (وهي) أي أفعال العباد (كلها ) أي جميعها من خيرها وشرها، وإن كانت مكاسبهم (بمشيئته) أي بإرادته وعلمه) أي بتعلق علمه وقضائه وقدره أي على وفق حكمه وطبق قدر تقديره، فهو مريد لما يسمى شراً من كفر ومعصية، كما هو مريد للخير من إيمان وطاعة. والطاعات (كلها أي جنسها بجميع أفرادها الشامل لواجبها وندبها (ما كانت) أي قليلة أو كثيرة (واجبةً) أي ثابتة بأمر الله تعالى) أي بإقامتها في الجملة حيث قال الله تعالى: وَأَطِيعُوا اللهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ ﴾ [المائدة : ۹۲] . وبمحبته) أي لقوله تعالى: فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يُحِبُّ الْمُتَّقِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٧٦]، وَاللهُ يُحِبُّ الْمُحْسِنِينَ [آل عمران : ١٣٤]، ﴿وَيُحِبُّ المتطهرين ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٢٢] (وبرضائه أي لقوله تعالى في حق المؤمنين : رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنْهُ ﴾ [البينة: ۸]، (وعلمه) أي لتعلق علمه سابقا في عالم الشهود، وتحققه لاحقاً في عالم الوجود (ومشيئته) أي بإرادته (وقضائه أي حكمه وتقديره) أي بمقدار قدّره أولاً، وكتبه في اللوح المحفوظ وحرّره ثانياً، وأظهره في عالم الكون وقرّره ثالثاً، ثم يجزيه جزاء وافيا في عالم العقبى رابعا. والمعاصى كلها أي صغيرها وكبيرها (بعلمه) وقضائه وتقديره ومشيئته إذ لو لم يردها لما وقعت (لا) بمحبته) أي لقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَإِنَّ اللهَ لَا يُحِبُّ الْكَفِرِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۳۲]، ﴿ وَاللهُ لا يُحِبُّ الظَّلِمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٥٧] ، ولا) (برضائه أي لقوله تعالى: وَلَا يَرْضى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ [الزمر: ۷]، ولأن الكفر يوجب المقت الذي هو أشد الغضب وهو ينافي رضا الرب المتعلق بالإيمان الأدب ولا) بأمره أي لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللهَ لَا يَأْمُرُ بِالْفَحْشَاءِ [الأعراف: ۲۸]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَانِ وَ إِيْتَ ي ذِي الْقُرْبى وَيَنْغَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنكَرِ وَالبَني [النحل :٩٠] فالنهى ضد الأمر فلا يتصور أن يكون الكفر بالأمر
And they (i.e., the actions of servants) are all by His will, His knowledge, His decree, and His predestination. The forms of acts of obedience are beloved to God, and acts of disobedience are predestined but not beloved. Acts of obedience, all of them, are what were obligatory by the command of God Most High, by His love, by His pleasure, by His knowledge, by His will, by His decree, and by His predestination. And acts of disobedience, all of them, are by His knowledge, His decree, His predestination, and His will. I said: That is because the action of the servant occurs according to his choice without any effect of his power concurrent with it. And it is supported by His saying, 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice. Exalted is God and high above what they associate [with Him].' [28:68]. Therefore, some of the knowers said: 'Do not choose, for if you must choose, choose not to choose.' (And they) meaning the actions of servants (all of them) meaning all of them, good and evil, even if they are their acquisitions, (are by His will) meaning His desire, (and His knowledge) meaning the attachment of His knowledge, (and His decree and His predestination) meaning in accordance with His judgment and according to the measure of His predestination. Thus He wills what is called evil, such as disbelief and disobedience, just as He wills good, such as faith and obedience. And acts of obedience (all of them meaning their genus with all its individuals, including obligatory and recommended, were) meaning few or many (obligatory) meaning established by the command of God Most High meaning establishing them in general, where God said: 'And obey God and obey the Messenger' [5:92]; (and by His love) meaning His saying: 'Indeed God loves the righteous' [3:76], 'And God loves the doers of good' [3:134], 'And He loves those who purify themselves' [2:222]; (and by His pleasure) meaning His saying concerning the believers: 'God is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him' [98:8]; (and by His knowledge) meaning the attachment of His knowledge previously in the world of witnessing and its realization later in the world of existence; (and by His will) meaning His desire; (and by His decree meaning His judgment and His predestination) meaning by a measure He determined first, wrote it in the Preserved Tablet and recorded it second, manifested it in the world of existence and established it third, then recompenses it with a full recompense in the world of the hereafter fourth. And acts of disobedience, all of them, small and great, (are by His knowledge, His decree, His predestination, and His will, for had He not willed them, they would not have occurred, (not) by His love) meaning His saying: 'Indeed God does not love the disbelievers' [3:32], 'And God does not love the wrongdoers' [3:57]; (and not) (by His pleasure) meaning His saying: 'And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants' [39:7], and because disbelief incurs wrath, which is the most severe anger, and it contradicts the pleasure of the Lord related to faith; (and not) (by His command) meaning His saying: 'Indeed God does not command immorality' [7:28], and His saying: 'Indeed God commands justice, good conduct, and giving to relatives, and forbids immorality, evil, and transgression' [16:90]. Thus prohibition is the opposite of command, so it is inconceivable that disbelief is by command.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition and proof of how human actions relate to God's attributes. The author begins by stating that all actions of servants—both good and evil—are by God's will, knowledge, decree, and predestination. He then clarifies that acts of obedience are beloved to God and occur by His command, love, pleasure, knowledge, will, decree, and predestination. Acts of disobedience, however, are only by His knowledge, decree, predestination, and will—not by His love, pleasure, or command.

The author provides scriptural evidence: for obedience, verses commanding obedience and affirming God's love for the righteous; for disobedience, verses stating that God does not love disbelievers or wrongdoers, that He is not pleased with disbelief, and that He does not command immorality. He also cites the principle that prohibition (nahy) is the opposite of command (amr), so disbelief cannot be by God's command.

The passage then expands on the meaning of each divine attribute in relation to actions: 'by His will' means His desire; 'by His knowledge' means the attachment of His eternal knowledge; 'by His decree and predestination' means His judgment and the measure determined, recorded in the Preserved Tablet, manifested in existence, and recompensed in the afterlife.

The author also notes a saying of some gnostics: 'Do not choose, for if you must choose, choose not to choose,' illustrating that true submission is to rely on God's choice rather than one's own.

This lesson establishes the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions, but moral responsibility and the distinction between obedience and disobedience are based on God's command and love, not merely on His will.

Key Terms
مشيئته (His will)
علمه (His knowledge)
قضاؤه (His decree)
قدره (His predestination)
محبته (His love)
رضاه (His pleasure)
أمره (His command)
طاعة (obedience)
معصية (disobedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God loves everything that He wills, including sinful actions.
Correction: God's will is universal, but His love and command are specific to good; He does not love disbelief or wrongdoing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT attributed to acts of disobedience?
A) God's knowledge
B) God's will
C) God's love
D) God's decree

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all human actions, but only obedience is by His love, pleasure, and command, while disobedience is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely believe that God hates that act, even though He willed it? How does that belief change the way you repent—or does it make you feel like your sin was 'meant to be' and therefore less your fault?
?
The gnostic said, 'Choose not to choose.' Is there a decision in your life right now where you are clinging to your own preference rather than surrendering to God's choice? What would it look like for you to truly submit in that area?
?
If God does not command evil, then every time you choose to disobey, you are acting directly against His command. How does that reality confront the excuses you make for your disobedience? Will you commit today to memorizing one of the verses in this passage (e.g., 'God does not command immorality') to remind yourself when temptation comes?

Proof that Sins Are Not by God's Love, Pleasure, or Command
Commentary on divine attributes negated from evil actions, with scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proofs that sins are not attributable to God's love, pleasure, or command, and the nuanced Salafi position on divine will and detailed attribution.

Core Idea
Sins are not by God's love, pleasure, or command, yet all things occur by His will.

This passage from the commentary establishes a crucial nuance in Islamic theology: while all events occur by God's will, specific divine attributes like love, pleasure, and command are not associated with sinful acts. The author cites clear Quranic verses and explains the Salaf's balanced approach to general and detailed attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three negations: love, pleasure, and command.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
For each negation, find the verse that the author quotes. Note how the verses directly negate these attributes from disbelievers, wrongdoers, and immoral acts.
The verse for command (An-Nahl 90) contrasts command with prohibition.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The author explains why disbelief cannot be by command: because God commands justice and forbids evil. Since prohibition is the opposite of command, evil cannot be commanded.
This is a logical argument based on Quranic text.
4
Grasp the Salafi position on attribution
The Salaf agreed that all things are willed by God in general, but some forbade detailed attribution (e.g., saying 'He wills disbelief') to avoid disrespect.
This shows how to balance divine transcendence with divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما وقعت (لا) بمحبته) أي لقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَإِنَّ اللهَ لَا يُحِبُّ الْكَفِرِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۳۲]، ﴿ وَاللهُ لا يُحِبُّ الظَّلِمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٥٧] ، ولا) (برضائه أي لقوله تعالى: وَلَا يَرْضى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ [الزمر: ۷] ، ولأن الكفر يوجب المقت الذي هو أشد الغضب وهو ينافي رضا الرب المتعلق بالإيمان الأدب ولا) بأمره أي لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللهَ لَا يَأْمُرُ بِالْفَحْشَاءِ [الأعراف: ۲۸]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَانِ وَ إِيْتَ ي ذِي الْقُرْبى وَيَنْغَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنكَرِ وَالبَني [النحل :٩٠] فالنهى ضد الأمر فلا يتصور أن يكون الكفر بالأمر، وهذا القول هو المعروف عن السلف، وقد اتفقوا على جواز إسناد الكل إليه سبحانه جملة، فيقال جميع الكائنات مرادة الله، ومنهم من منع التفصيل فقال: لا يقال إنه يريد الكفر والظلم والفسق لإيهامه الكفر ولرعاية الأدب سبحانه كما يقال خالق الأشياء، ولا يقال خالق القاذورات.
When he said (not by His love) i.e. by His saying the Most High: 'Indeed Allah does not love the disbelievers' (Al 'Imran 32), 'And Allah does not love the wrongdoers' (Al 'Imran 57), and (not by His pleasure) i.e. by His saying the Most High: 'He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants' (Az-Zumar 7), and because disbelief necessitates wrath which is intense anger and it contradicts the pleasure of the Lord related to faith and good conduct, and (not by His command) i.e. by His saying the Most High: 'Indeed Allah does not command immorality' (Al-A'raf 28), and His saying the Most High: 'Indeed Allah commands justice and good conduct and giving to relatives, and forbids immorality and wrongdoing and oppression' (An-Nahl 90), so prohibition is the opposite of command, so it is inconceivable that disbelief occurs by command. And this saying is the well-known one from the Salaf, and they agreed on the permissibility of attributing everything to Him the Exalted in general, so one says all things are willed by Allah. And among them are those who forbade detailed attribution, saying: one must not say He wills disbelief, injustice, and sin because it suggests evil and out of reverence for the proper conduct towards the Exalted, just as one says Creator of things, and one does not say Creator of filth.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the negation of certain divine attributes from evil actions. The author clarifies the phrase 'not by His love, nor by His pleasure, nor by His command' by citing relevant Qur'anic verses. For love, he quotes verses stating Allah does not love disbelievers and wrongdoers. For pleasure, he quotes that Allah is not pleased with disbelief for His servants and explains that disbelief incurs wrath which is opposite of the pleasure associated with faith. For command, he quotes verses that Allah does not command immorality and that He commands justice and forbids evil; since prohibition is the opposite of command, evil cannot be by His command. The author then states that this is the known position of the Salaf (early pious Muslims). They agreed that all things can be attributed to Allah generally (i.e., all things are willed by Him), but some forbid detailed attribution (e.g., saying 'God wills evil') to avoid implying evil in the divine, observing proper reverence, just as we say 'Creator of things' but not 'Creator of filth.' The passage thus establishes a nuanced position: all events occur by God's will, but specific attributes like love, pleasure, and command are not associated with evil.

Key Terms
حب
رضا
أمر
سلف
تفصيل
إجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that denying God's love, pleasure, and command for sin means sin is independent of God's will.
Correction: The Salaf affirmed that all things occur by God's will in general, while negating these specific attributes from sinful acts to maintain divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say disbelief cannot be by God's command?
A) Because disbelief is not a created act.
B) Because God commands justice and forbids evil, and prohibition opposes command.
C) Because God does not will disbelief at all.
D) Because the Salaf forbade all attribution of actions to God.

Key Takeaway
The Quran and Salaf agree that sins occur by God's will but not by His love, pleasure, or command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes tell yourself that 'God willed it' without also remembering that He does not love or command it? How does this lesson force you to face your own responsibility and God's absolute perfection?
?
The Salaf avoided saying 'God wills sin' out of reverence for Allah. In your daily conversations, do you ever casually attribute evil or suffering to Allah in a way that might fall short of proper adab? What will you change in your speech starting today?
?
If God neither loves nor commands the evil that happens, yet it occurs under His will, how does that reshape your understanding of His justice and your own repentance? Will you now repent with deeper humility, knowing that He permits but does not approve?

Classification of Works and Divine Attributes for Obligatory Acts
Classification of works and attributes for obligatory acts, with mashiyya/irada distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how works are classified into obligatory, virtue, and sin, and which divine attributes are associated with obligatory acts, along with the subtle distinction between mashiyya and irada.

Core Idea
Actions are not all equal; each type has a unique relationship with God's attributes.

This passage from Al-Fiqh al-Akbar commentary begins by classifying human works into three categories: obligatory, virtue, and sin. It then elaborates on the divine attributes that pertain to obligatory acts, including a crucial clarification between two terms for 'will'—mashiyya and irada. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly attributing actions to God without implying injustice or imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning. Note the classification of works and the list of attributes.
Pay special attention to the distinction between mashiyya and irada.
2
Identify the Three Categories
From the first sentences, find the three categories of works and their definitions: farida, fadila, and ma'siya.
Remember that farida includes both belief and deed, or deed alone.
3
Analyze Attributes for Obligatory Acts
List the divine attributes mentioned for farida: command, mashiyya, love, pleasure, decree, predestination, irada, success-giving, creation, etc. Then focus on the explanation of mashiyya vs irada and the redundancy of hukm.
The author says mashiyya is eternal, irada relates to the act in existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن شارحاً حلّ عبارة الإمام على أن الطاعات والمعاصي مفعولات ليخلق، وأن قوله واجبة خبر ما كانت مندوبة، والأولى ما قرّرنا، وعلى عموم معنى الأمر حرّرنا، والمسألة مبسوطة في 'الوصية' حيث قال: نقر بأن الأعمال ثلاثة: فريضة أي اعتقادا وعملا، أو عملا لا اعتقادا ليشمل الواجب وفضيلة أي سنة أو مستحبة أو نافلة ومعصية أي حرام أو مكروه، فالفريضة بأمر الله تعالى ومشيئته ومحبته ورضاه وقضائه وتقديره وإرادته وتوفيقه وتخليقه أي خلق فعله وفق حكمه فهو تفسير لما قبله. وأما قوله وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ فظاهر العبارة هو التفرقة بين المشيئة والإرادة، فالمشيئة أزلية في المرتبة الشهودية والإرادة تعلقها بالفعل في الحالة الوجودية، هذا ما سنح لي في هذا المقام، والله تعالى أعلم بمرام الإمام. وكذا الحكم يظهر أنه مستدرك؛ لأنه إما أن يراد به الحكم الأزلي فهو بمعنى القضاء الأولى أو يراد به الأمر الكوني في عالم الظهور الخلقي فقد تقدم ذكر الأمر بهذا المعنى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنهما كالتأكيد والتأييد في المبنى. ثم قوله والفضيلة ليست بأمر الله تعالى، أي بالأمر الموجب قطعاً أو ظناً، وإلا فهي داخلة في ذلك الأمر المقتضي استحسانا، وكذا مندرج في قوله، ولكن بمشيئته ومحبته ورضائه وقضائه وتقديره وتوفيقه وتخليقه وإرادته وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ. فنؤمن باللوح والقلم وبجميع ما فيه، والمعصية ليست بأمر الله ولكن بمشيئته لا بمحبته، وبقضائه لا برضائه، وبتقديره وتخليقه لا بتوفيقه وبخذلانه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ، انتهى.
Then know that a commentator interpreted the Imam's statement that acts of obedience and disobedience are created things (maf'ulat li-khalq), and that his saying 'obligatory' (wajiba) is predicated of what was recommended (manduba), and the more correct is what we have established, and we have elaborated on the general meaning of command. The issue is elaborated in 'Al-Wasiyya' where he said: We affirm that works are three: obligatory (farida), i.e. in belief and deed, or in deed not belief, to include the compulsory (wajib); and virtue (fadila), i.e. sunna or recommended or supererogatory; and sin (ma'siya), i.e. prohibited or disliked. So the obligatory (al-farida) is by the command of Allah Most High, and His will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irada), success-giving, and creation (takhliq) i.e. creating the act according to His judgment, and that is an explanation of what preceded. And as for His statement 'and His judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet', the apparent wording is a distinction between mashiyya (will) and irada (will/desire): mashiyya is eternal in the rank of witnessing, and irada attaches to the act in the state of existence. This is what occurred to me in this context, and Allah Most High knows best the intent of the Imam. Likewise, the judgment (al-hukm) appears to be redundant; because if what is meant is the eternal judgment, it is synonymous with the first decree (al-qada' al-awwal), or if what is meant is the existential command (al-amr al-kawni) in the world of manifest creation, then the mention of command has already preceded in this sense, unless one says they are like reinforcement (ta'kid) and corroboration (ta'yid) in meaning. Then his saying that the virtue (al-fadila) is not by the command of Allah Most High, i.e. by the command that is binding (al-amr al-mujib) definitely or probably; otherwise it is included in that command which implies approval, and likewise it is included in his saying, but rather it is by His will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, success-giving, creation, desire (irada), judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet. So we believe in the Tablet and the Pen and all that is in it. And sin is not by the command of Allah but by His will (mashiyya) not by His love, and by His decree not by His pleasure, and by His predestination and creation not by His success-giving, but by His abandonment (khidhlan) and His knowledge and writing in the Preserved Tablet. End.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a classification and theological analysis of human actions in relation to divine attributes. The author begins by referencing a commentator on Imam Abu Hanifa's statement about deeds being created. He then elaborates on the classification from 'Al-Wasiyya': works are three—obligatory (farida, including belief and deed or deed alone), virtue (fadila, including sunna, recommended, and supererogatory), and sin (ma'siya, including prohibited and disliked). Next, he details divine attributes associated with each category. Obligatory acts are attributed to God's command, will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irada), success-giving, and creation (takhliq). The author then clarifies a distinction within the attributes: the mention of 'judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet' points to a differentiation between 'mashiyya' and 'irada'. He defines mashiyya as eternal in the 'rank of witnessing' (presumably the divine essence) and irada as relating to the act in existence. He considers the inclusion of 'judgment' (hukm) as possibly redundant, explaining it could be the eternal judgment (like the first decree) or the existential command in creation. Virtue (fadila) is not by a binding command but is included under a broader command that implies approval; it does have all other attributes (will, love, pleasure, etc.). Sin (ma'siya) is specifically not by God's command, nor by His love or pleasure, but by His will (mashiyya), decree (qada'), predestination (taqdir), and creation (takhliq) without success-giving; instead, it is by His abandonment (khidhlan). The passage ends with affirmation of belief in the Tablet and Pen, and the distinction that sin is by God's will and knowledge but not by His love, pleasure, or command.

Key Terms
فريضة
فضيلة
معصية
مشيئة
إرادة
تخليق
توفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mashiyya and irada are the same divine attribute with no distinction.
Correction: Mashiyya is eternal in the divine essence (rank of witnessing), while irada attaches to the act in existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author distinguish mashiyya from irada?
A) Mashiyya is eternal, irada is temporal.
B) Mashiyya relates to the act, irada is eternal.
C) Mashiyya is in the Preserved Tablet, irada is in the divine essence.
D) They are synonyms with no difference.

Key Takeaway
Works are classified into three, and obligatory acts are linked to a full set of divine attributes, with an important distinction between God's eternal will (mashiyya) and His existential desire (irada).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God does not love that act? How does that awareness shape the sincerity of your repentance?
?
The passage distinguishes God's eternal will from His existential desire. Do you ever mistake what God has decreed for what He delights in? Recall a situation where you assumed God's will meant His approval. How can you align your heart with His pleasure, not just His decree?
?
Obligatory acts are linked to God's love, pleasure, and tawfīq. Do you perform your obligations seeking His success or relying on your own strength? What practical step can you take today to rely on His tawfīq in a specific duty?

Divine Attributes for Virtue and Sin and Belief in the Tablet
Divine attributes for virtue and sin, and belief in the Tablet and Pen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attributes associated with virtuous acts and sinful acts, and the specific attributes that are negated for sin, as well as the belief in the Prescribed Tablet and the Pen.

Core Idea
Virtue and sin share some divine attributes but differ in key ones like command, love, and success-giving.

Having discussed obligatory acts, the passage now turns to virtue (fadila) and sin (ma'siya). It explains that virtue is not under God's binding command but still involves His will, love, pleasure, and other attributes. Sin, on the other hand, is by God's will and decree but not by His love, pleasure, or success-giving; instead, it involves His abandonment. The passage concludes with affirmation of belief in the Preserved Tablet and the Pen, which record all divine decrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the Second Part of the Passage
Read the Arabic text for virtue and sin along with the translation. Notice the careful wording: 'not by His command' for virtue, and multiple negations for sin.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا بمحبته' for sin.
2
Identify Attributes for Virtue
List the attributes that virtue does have: it is by His will, love, pleasure, etc. But note: it is not by His command in the binding sense. Understanding this nuance is important.
The passage says virtue is included under the command that implies approval, not a binding command.
3
Identify Attributes for Sin
Sin is by God's will and decree but not by His love, pleasure, or success-giving. It involves His abandonment (khidhlan). Notice the precise pairing: by His will, not by His love; by His decree, not by His pleasure; by His predestination and creation, not by His success-giving.
This shows that sin is still within God's overall will and decree, but without His approval.
4
Reflect on Belief in the Tablet and Pen
The passage ends with affirming belief in the Preserved Tablet and the Pen and everything in them. This underscores that all actions, including sin, are recorded by God's knowledge.
This is a core tenet of Sunni faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن شارحاً حلّ عبارة الإمام على أن الطاعات والمعاصي مفعولات ليخلق، وأن قوله واجبة خبر ما كانت مندوبة، والأولى ما قرّرنا، وعلى عموم معنى الأمر حرّرنا، والمسألة مبسوطة في 'الوصية' حيث قال: نقر بأن الأعمال ثلاثة: فريضة أي اعتقادا وعملا، أو عملا لا اعتقادا ليشمل الواجب وفضيلة أي سنة أو مستحبة أو نافلة ومعصية أي حرام أو مكروه، فالفريضة بأمر الله تعالى ومشيئته ومحبته ورضاه وقضائه وتقديره وإرادته وتوفيقه وتخليقه أي خلق فعله وفق حكمه فهو تفسير لما قبله. وأما قوله وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ فظاهر العبارة هو التفرقة بين المشيئة والإرادة، فالمشيئة أزلية في المرتبة الشهودية والإرادة تعلقها بالفعل في الحالة الوجودية، هذا ما سنح لي في هذا المقام، والله تعالى أعلم بمرام الإمام. وكذا الحكم يظهر أنه مستدرك؛ لأنه إما أن يراد به الحكم الأزلي فهو بمعنى القضاء الأولى أو يراد به الأمر الكوني في عالم الظهور الخلقي فقد تقدم ذكر الأمر بهذا المعنى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنهما كالتأكيد والتأييد في المبنى. ثم قوله والفضيلة ليست بأمر الله تعالى، أي بالأمر الموجب قطعاً أو ظناً، وإلا فهي داخلة في ذلك الأمر المقتضي استحسانا، وكذا مندرج في قوله، ولكن بمشيئته ومحبته ورضائه وقضائه وتقديره وتوفيقه وتخليقه وإرادته وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ. فنؤمن باللوح والقلم وبجميع ما فيه، والمعصية ليست بأمر الله ولكن بمشيئته لا بمحبته، وبقضائه لا برضائه، وبتقديره وتخليقه لا بتوفيقه وبخذلانه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ، انتهى.
Then know that a commentator interpreted the Imam's statement that acts of obedience and disobedience are created things (maf'ulat li-khalq), and that his saying 'obligatory' (wajiba) is predicated of what was recommended (manduba), and the more correct is what we have established, and we have elaborated on the general meaning of command. The issue is elaborated in 'Al-Wasiyya' where he said: We affirm that works are three: obligatory (farida), i.e. in belief and deed, or in deed not belief, to include the compulsory (wajib); and virtue (fadila), i.e. sunna or recommended or supererogatory; and sin (ma'siya), i.e. prohibited or disliked. So the obligatory (al-farida) is by the command of Allah Most High, and His will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irada), success-giving, and creation (takhliq) i.e. creating the act according to His judgment, and that is an explanation of what preceded. And as for His statement 'and His judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet', the apparent wording is a distinction between mashiyya (will) and irada (will/desire): mashiyya is eternal in the rank of witnessing, and irada attaches to the act in the state of existence. This is what occurred to me in this context, and Allah Most High knows best the intent of the Imam. Likewise, the judgment (al-hukm) appears to be redundant; because if what is meant is the eternal judgment, it is synonymous with the first decree (al-qada' al-awwal), or if what is meant is the existential command (al-amr al-kawni) in the world of manifest creation, then the mention of command has already preceded in this sense, unless one says they are like reinforcement (ta'kid) and corroboration (ta'yid) in meaning. Then his saying that the virtue (al-fadila) is not by the command of Allah Most High, i.e. by the command that is binding (al-amr al-mujib) definitely or probably; otherwise it is included in that command which implies approval, and likewise it is included in his saying, but rather it is by His will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, success-giving, creation, desire (irada), judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet. So we believe in the Tablet and the Pen and all that is in it. And sin is not by the command of Allah but by His will (mashiyya) not by His love, and by His decree not by His pleasure, and by His predestination and creation not by His success-giving, but by His abandonment (khidhlan) and His knowledge and writing in the Preserved Tablet. End.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a classification and theological analysis of human actions in relation to divine attributes. The author begins by referencing a commentator on Imam Abu Hanifa's statement about deeds being created. He then elaborates on the classification from 'Al-Wasiyya': works are three—obligatory (farida, including belief and deed or deed alone), virtue (fadila, including sunna, recommended, and supererogatory), and sin (ma'siya, including prohibited and disliked). Next, he details divine attributes associated with each category. Obligatory acts are attributed to God's command, will (mashiyya), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irada), success-giving, and creation (takhliq). The author then clarifies a distinction within the attributes: the mention of 'judgment, knowledge, and writing in the Preserved Tablet' points to a differentiation between 'mashiyya' and 'irada'. He defines mashiyya as eternal in the 'rank of witnessing' (presumably the divine essence) and irada as relating to the act in existence. He considers the inclusion of 'judgment' (hukm) as possibly redundant, explaining it could be the eternal judgment (like the first decree) or the existential command in creation. Virtue (fadila) is not by a binding command but is included under a broader command that implies approval; it does have all other attributes (will, love, pleasure, etc.). Sin (ma'siya) is specifically not by God's command, nor by His love or pleasure, but by His will (mashiyya), decree (qada'), predestination (taqdir), and creation (takhliq) without success-giving; instead, it is by His abandonment (khidhlan). The passage ends with affirmation of belief in the Tablet and Pen, and the distinction that sin is by God's will and knowledge but not by His love, pleasure, or command.

Key Terms
فضيلة
معصية
خذلان
لوح محفوظ
قلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sin is outside God's will and decree, implying that evil occurs without God's permission.
Correction: Sin occurs by God's will and decree, but it is not by His love, pleasure, or success-giving; it involves His abandonment.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT attributed to sin according to the passage?
A) God's will (mashiyya).
B) God's decree (qada').
C) God's love (mahabbah).
D) God's abandonment (khidhlan).

Key Takeaway
Virtue is under God's approval but not binding command, while sin occurs by God's will and decree but without His love, pleasure, or support; all is recorded in the Preserved Tablet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you assume God still loves and approves of your action, or do you recognize that He abandons you to it? How does this truth change the way you seek repentance?
?
Virtue is associated with God's love and success-giving. Are you actively seeking His success (tawfiq) through your good deeds, or do you rely on your own strength? What would it look like to depend on Him for every virtuous act?
?
This lesson says that all things are written in the Preserved Tablet. Does this knowledge make you more humble and reliant on God, or do you use it as an excuse to avoid personal responsibility? What is one step you can take today to act with the awareness that your actions are recorded but you are still accountable?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Divine Will and Evil
Author refutes Mu'tazila claim that God does not will evil using verses and a story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that God's will encompasses all things, including evil, and why willing evil is not attributable to God in the same way as to humans.

Core Idea
God's will is absolute; nothing occurs in His dominion except by His will.

The Mu'tazila argued that God does not will evil because willing evil is itself evil. This lesson presents the Sunni refutation using Qur'anic verses, a hadith, and a logical distinction between divine and human willing. A famous debate illustrates the Sunni position that God is the absolute Owner and is not questioned about His actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the first part of the passage to understand what the Mu'tazila believe: that evil acts are evil in themselves and thus cannot be willed by God.
Notice the phrasing 'صور القبيح قبيحة'.
2
Examine the scriptural proofs
Look at the Qur'anic verses and the hadith cited. Each one affirms that God's will extends to guidance and misguidance, and that disobedience occurs only by His will.
The hadith about Iblis is particularly striking.
3
Understand the rational counter-argument
Read the author's response to the Mu'tazila principle 'willing evil is evil'. Note the distinction: it applies to humans but not to God, because His will is always coupled with wisdom and He is the absolute Owner.
4
Analyze the debate story
Read the story of Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini. See how the Sunni scholar defends God's sovereignty by affirming that whatever occurs in His dominion is by His will and that He does not owe anything to anyone.
The Qadi's final question about prevention is answered by distinguishing between what is 'yours' and what is 'His'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور القبيح قبيحة، فعندهم يكون أكثر ما يقع من أفعال العباد على خلاف إرادة الله سبحانه. وقد دلّت الآيات الواضحات على خلاف قولهم، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحُ صَدْرَةَ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَنْ يُرِدْ اَنْ يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَا ضَيِّقًا حَرَ جَاه [الأنعام: ١٢٥]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿انلو يَشَاءُ اللَّهُ لَهَدَى النَّاسَ جَمِيعًا [الرعد: ۳۱] ، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْشتُنَا لَاتَيْنَاكُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدُهَا [السجدة :١٣]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا تَشَاءُونَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ ﴾ [الإنسان : ۳۰]. وروى البيهقي بسنده أن النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لأبي بكر رضي الله عنه: (لو أراد الله أن لا يُعصى ما خلق إبليس)) . ثم قول المعتزلة : إرادة القبيح قبيحة» هو بالنسبة إلينا أما بالنسبة إلى الله سبحانه فليست كذلك؛ فإنها قد تكون مقرونة بحكمة تقتضي هنالك؛ مع أنه مالك الأمور على الإطلاق، كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿ يَفْعَلُ اللهُ مَا يَشَاءُ [إبراهيم: ۲۷] ، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ اللهَ يَحْكُمُ مَا يُرِيدُ [المائدة: ١]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يُسْتَلْ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣].

وحكي أن القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني أحد الشيوخ المعتزلة دخل على الصاحب بن عباد وعنده الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرائيني أحد أئمة أهل السنة فلما رأى الأستاذ قال: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء، فقال الأستاذ فورا: سبحان من لا يقع في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال القاضي: أيشاء ربنا أن يعصى؟ قال الأستاذ : أيعصى ربنا قهراً؟ فقال القاضي: أرأيت إن منعني وقضى عليّ بالردى أحسن إلي أم أساء؟ فقال الأستاذ: إن منعك ما هو لك فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يختص برحمته من يشاء، فبهت القاضي.
Forms of evil are evil, thus according to them most of what occurs from the actions of servants is contrary to the will of Allah, Exalted is He. And clear verses have indicated contrary to their statement, such as His saying, the Exalted: {For whomever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whomever He wants to lead astray, He makes his breast tight and constricted} [Al-An'am 6:125], and His saying, the Exalted: {If Allah had willed, He would have guided all people} [Ar-Ra'd 13:31], and His saying, the Exalted: {And if We willed, We could have given every soul its guidance} [As-Sajdah 32:13], and His saying, the Exalted: {And you do not will unless Allah wills} [Al-Insan 76:30]. And Al-Bayhaqi narrated with his chain that the Prophet ﷺ said to Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him: 'If Allah had willed not to be disobeyed, He would not have created Iblis.' Then the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'Willing evil is evil' is relative to us, but with respect to Allah, Exalted is He, it is not so; for it may be coupled with a wisdom that necessitates it there; moreover, He is the absolute Owner of all affairs, as Allah, the Exalted, said: {Allah does what He wills} [Ibrahim 14:27], and His saying, the Exalted: {Indeed, Allah rules what He intends} [Al-Ma'idah 5:1], and His saying, the Exalted: {He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned} [Al-Anbiya' 21:23]. And it is related that Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani, one of the Mu'tazila shaykhs, entered upon al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad while with him was the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini, one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunnah. When he saw the master, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is transcendent above indecency.' The master immediately said: 'Glory be to the One from whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Qadi said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The master said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Qadi said: 'If He prevents me and decrees destruction upon me, has He done good to me or evil?' The master said: 'If He prevents what is yours, then He has done evil; but if He prevents what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Qadi was confounded.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God does not will evil acts and that willing evil is itself evil. The author first states the Mu'tazila view: evil acts are evil in themselves, and since God is good, most human actions occur contrary to His will. The author then cites several Qur'anic verses to prove that God's will encompasses all things, including guidance and misguidance. A hadith is also cited: if God had willed not to be disobeyed, He would not have created Iblis. The author then responds to the Mu'tazila argument that 'willing evil is evil' by distinguishing between human and divine willing: for humans, willing evil may be evil, but for God, it is not, because His will is always coupled with wisdom and He is the absolute Owner. The passage concludes with a story of a debate between Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar (Mu'tazila) and Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini (Sunni). The Qadi's questions are met with answers that uphold God's sovereignty: whatever occurs in His dominion is by His will, and He does not owe anything to anyone; He is the owner of all things. The story illustrates the Sunni position that God's will is comprehensive and not subject to human standards of good and evil.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
قبيح
المعتزلة
قضاء
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then God is evil.
Correction: Divine willing is not comparable to human willing; God's will is always coupled with wisdom, and as the absolute Owner, He is not subject to human moral categories.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the story about Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfaraini?
A) It shows that God does not will evil.
B) It demonstrates that God is the absolute owner and whatever occurs in His dominion is by His will.
C) It proves that humans have free will independent of God's will.
D) It refutes the use of reasoning in theology.

Key Takeaway
God's will is comprehensive and not subject to human moral judgments; He is the absolute Owner and His actions are always wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly submit to God's will even when it includes things you consider evil or unjust in your life? Where do you find yourself resisting His decree, and what would it take to surrender to His absolute ownership?
?
If God's actions are not judged by human standards, how does that challenge your sense of fairness? Can you accept that His wisdom may be hidden from you, and that He is free to give or withhold as He wills without being questioned?
?
When you face hardship or see suffering, do you say 'God does what He wills' with contentment, or do you question His justice? What practical steps can you take today to deepen your trust in His will and wisdom, even when it is painful?

Defining Good and Evil in Light of God's Will and Pleasure
Author defines good and evil acts in terms of God's pleasure and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between God's will and His pleasure regarding good and evil acts, and why this distinction matters.

Core Idea
God wills everything, but is pleased only with good.

In this passage, the author defines what makes an act good or evil in Islamic theology, clarifying that while God's will and decree encompass all actions, His pleasure and command are exclusive to good deeds. This distinction resolves a common confusion about divine responsibility for evil and is supported by a key Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the concepts before analyzing them.
Pay attention to the terms 'will' (irada), 'pleasure' (rida), and 'decree' (qada).
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate where the author defines 'good' (al-hasan) and 'evil' (al-qabih). Notice that both are defined in terms of worldly and otherworldly consequences, but also in relation to God's pleasure and will.
3
Analyze the distinction
Understand how the author distinguishes between God's will/decree and His pleasure/command. The verse from Surah Az-Zumar provides the proof that God is not pleased with disbelief, even though it occurs by His will.
This distinction is central to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومجمل الكلام في تحصيل المرام إن الحسن من أفعال العباد وهو ما يكون متعلق المدحة في الدنيا والمثوبة في العقبى برضاء الله تعالى وإرادته وقضائه والقبيح منها، وهو ما يكون متعلق المذمة في العاجل والعقوبة في الآجل، ليس برضائه، بل بإرادته وقضائه لقوله سبحانه: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ﴾ [الزمر: ۷]، فالإرادة والمشيئة والتقدير تتعلق بالكل، والرضا والمحبة والأمر لا تتعلق إلا بالحسن دون القبيح من الفعل، حيث أمرهم بالإيمان مع تقرّر علمه بأنهم يموتون على الكفر.
And the sum of the speech in achieving the goal is that the good from the actions of servants is what is connected to praise in this world and reward in the afterlife, by the pleasure of Allah, the Exalted, and His will and His decree; and the evil from them is what is connected to blame in the immediate and punishment in the later, not by His pleasure, but by His will and His decree, because of His saying, the Exalted: {And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants} [Az-Zumar 39:7]. So will, volition, and predestination pertain to all, while pleasure, love, and command pertain only to the good, not the evil of action, since He commanded them to believe while His knowledge was established that they would die upon disbelief.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of good and evil acts from an Ash'ari perspective. The author defines 'good' (al-hasan) as that which is connected to praise in this world and reward in the afterlife, based on God's pleasure (rida'), will (irada), and decree (qada'). 'Evil' (al-qabih) is defined as that which is connected to blame in this life and punishment in the afterlife, and it is not by God's pleasure but by His will and decree. The author then distinguishes between two categories: God's will and decree encompass all actions, both good and evil. But God's pleasure, love, and command only attach to good actions. This is demonstrated by the fact that God commanded faith to all, even though He knew some would die in disbelief. Thus, God's command does not imply His pleasure with the act of disbelief; rather, disbelief occurs by His will but not by His pleasure.

Key Terms
حسن
قبيح
إرادة
رضا
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think that if God wills an act, He must also be pleased with it, leading to the conclusion that God approves of evil.
Correction: The author clarifies that God's will and decree encompass all events, but His pleasure, love, and command are limited to good actions only. Evil acts occur by God's will but not by His pleasure.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between God's will and evil acts?
A) God does not will evil acts at all.
B) God wills evil acts and is also pleased with them.
C) God wills evil acts but is not pleased with them.
D) Evil acts occur without any connection to God's will.

Key Takeaway
God wills all things, but His pleasure and command are reserved only for what is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'it was God's will'? How does this lesson force you to see that God's will does not equal His pleasure? Will you now distinguish between what God allows and what He loves, and take full responsibility for your choices?
?
God commands belief but knows some will die in disbelief. Does this make God unjust in your heart? What does your honest answer reveal about your understanding of divine sovereignty and human accountability? Are you willing to submit to a God who wills both good and evil but only loves the good?
?
If God is not pleased with evil, yet it occurs by His will, what is your response when you witness evil in the world? Do you blame God or see it as part of a greater wisdom? This week, when you face something evil, will you remember that God does not approve of it — and that you are called to stand against it, not resign to it?

God's Will vs. His Pleasure
Distinguishes God's will from His pleasure in relation to good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's universal will and His pleasure, and why evil is willed but not approved.

Core Idea
God wills everything that happens, but only loves and approves the good.

One of the most subtle distinctions in Islamic theology is between what God wills (which covers all existence) and what God is pleased with (which only covers good actions). This passage from al-Fiqh al-Akbar clarifies this distinction using the Quranic verse that God is not pleased with disbelief for His servants. Read carefully to grasp how will and pleasure relate to good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice that the passage starts by linking reward in the afterlife to God's pleasure, will, and decree.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides actions into good (reward) and evil (blame). Find how each is related to God's attributes.
3
Understand the Quranic proof
The passage quotes Quran 39:7 to show that God is not pleased with disbelief. Reflect on why this verse is used to prove the distinction.
4
Summarize the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that will and decree pertain to everything, while pleasure, love, and command only pertain to good actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدنيا والمثوبة في العقبى برضاء الله تعالى وإرادته وقضائه والقبيح منها، وهو ما يكون متعلق المذمة في العاجل والعقوبة في الآجل، ليس برضائه، بل بإرادته وقضائه لقوله سبحانه: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَىٰ لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ﴾ [الزمر: 7]، فالإرادة والمشيئة والتقدير تتعلق بالكل، والرضا والمحبة والأمر لا تتعلق إلا بالحسن دون القبيح من الفعل.
In this world and the reward in the afterlife are by the pleasure of Allah Most High, His will and His decree. And the ugly [action] is that which is the object of blame in the immediate and punishment in the future; it is not by His pleasure, but by His will and decree, because of His saying, Exalted is He: 'And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants.' So will, intention, and decree pertain to everything, while pleasure, love, and command pertain only to the good, not the ugly of action.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of God's will (إرادة) and pleasure (رضا). It states that evil actions are not willed with pleasure but are willed in the sense of being decreed. The author uses Quran 39:7 to support that God is not pleased with disbelief. Then concludes that will and decree encompass all, while pleasure, love, and command only attach to good actions.

Correction: The passage clarifies that evil actions are willed and decreed by God, but He is not pleased with them; pleasure only attaches to good.

Checkpoint

B) Love (محبة)

Key Takeaway
God's will and decree apply to all actions, good and evil, but His pleasure, love, and command only apply to good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Meaning of 'His Chosen One'
Author explains meanings of 'his chosen one'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the Prophet being described as Allah's chosen one (مصطفاه and منتقاه) and the implications of his central role in creation.

Core Idea
The Prophet is Allah's chosen one, selected with miracles and stations beyond all creation.

This passage begins by unpacking the phrase 'His chosen one' (وصفيه) from the creed. The author explains that it refers to the Prophet being specially selected by Allah with various honors and elevated stations. A variant reading adds 'his elect' (منتقاه), emphasizing that Allah chose him from among all creatures. The famous poetic line 'Lolāka' (Without you) is cited to indicate that the universe was created for his sake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how the author defines 'His chosen one' and mentions the variant.
2
Understand the poet's reference
The passage refers to the line 'Lolāka' from al-Busiri's Burdah. Consider how this highlights the Prophet's centrality.
This is a well-known reference in Islamic tradition.
3
Connect to sinlessness
The passage then links to the statement that the Prophet never worshipped an idol. This sets the stage for discussing his sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وصفيه) أي مصطفاه بأنواع من الكرامات وحقائق المقامات الدنيوية والأخروية. وفي نسخة بزيادة ومنتقاه أي مختاره ومجتباه من بين مخلوقاته كما يشير إليه قول القائل: لولاه من قصيدة البردة للبوصيري. لم تخرج الدنيا من العدم. ولم يعبد الصنم أي ولا غيره، لقوله: (ولم يشرك بالله طرفة عين قط) أي لا قبل النبوة ولا بعدها، فإن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام معصومون عن الكفر مطلقا بالإجماع، وإن جوز بعضهم صدور الصغيرة بل الكبيرة قبل النبوة بل بعدها أيضاً في مقام النزاع، وأما هو صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم فكما قال الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله (ولم يرتكب صغيرة ولا كبيرة قط) وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿عَفَا اللهُ عَنْكَ لِمَ اَذِنْتَ لَهُمْ ﴾ الآية [التوبة:٤٣]، وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿ما كَانَ لِنَبِي أَن يَكُونَ لَةَ أَسْرى الله الآية [الأنفال:٦٧] فمحمول على ترك الأولى بالنسبة إلى مقامه الأعلى.
(And his chosen one) meaning his selected one with kinds of miracles and realities of stations, worldly and otherworldly. In one copy there is an addition: 'and his elect' meaning his chosen and preferred one from among His creatures, as indicated by the saying of the poet: 'Without him, the world would not have emerged from non-existence.' 'And he did not worship an idol' i.e., nor anything else, because of his statement: 'And he did not associate any partner with Allah for the blink of an eye ever' i.e., neither before prophethood nor after it. For the prophets, peace be upon them, are protected from disbelief absolutely by consensus, although some permitted the occurrence of minor or even major sins before prophethood and even after it in the context of dispute. As for him (the Prophet), as the Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And he never committed a minor or major sin ever.' As for His statement: 'Allah has forgiven you; why did you give them permission?' [9:43], and similarly His statement: 'It is not for a prophet to have captives' [8:67], it is interpreted as leaving what is better relative to his exalted station.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage explains two attributes of the Prophet: being chosen (مصطفى) and being sinless (عصمة). It first clarifies the meaning of 'his chosen one' (وصفيه) as one selected with various miracles and stations. A variant reading adds 'his elect' (منتقاه). The poet's line 'Lolāka' (without you) is cited to indicate the Prophet's central role in creation. Then it explains the statement that the Prophet never worshipped an idol or associated partners with Allah. It affirms the consensus on prophetic infallibility from disbelief. While some scholars allowed possibility of minor or major sins before prophethood, the Imam (Abu Hanifa) asserts that the Prophet never committed any sin, small or great. The verses that might appear to rebuke the Prophet (such as Surah At-Tawba 9:43 and Al-Anfal 8:67) are interpreted as leaving what is better (ترك الأولى) for his supreme station.

Key Terms
وصفيه
مصطفاه
منتقاه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Prophet's mercy
B) The Prophet being chosen and preferred from among all creatures
C) The Prophet's knowledge
D) The Prophet's wealth

Key Takeaway
The Prophet is Allah's chosen one with special miracles and stations, and his central role is indicated by the saying 'Without him, the world would not have emerged from non-existence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Prophet never associated anyone with Allah even for a blink of an eye. Search your heart: what or who do you sometimes put above Allah in your thoughts, desires, or actions? What concrete step will you take today to correct that?

The Prophet's Sinlessness (عصمة)
Author affirms the Prophet's sinlessness and interprets problematic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine of the Prophet's sinlessness, including the consensus on protection from disbelief and the interpretation of apparent rebukes in the Quran.

Core Idea
The Prophet never committed any sin, big or small, and Quranic verses that seem to rebuke him actually refer to leaving what is better.

This passage continues the discussion of the Prophet's attributes by focusing on his sinlessness. The author affirms that prophets are protected from disbelief by consensus, and specifically for the Prophet Muhammad, he never committed a minor or major sin. The passage then addresses two Quranic verses that might appear to criticize the Prophet, explaining that they are understood as 'leaving what is better' (ترك الأولى) for his lofty station.

Refutation of Taftazani's Error on Forgiveness
Critique of Taftazani's slip concerning forgiveness with or without repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani's claim that Allah can forgive sins after repentance is considered a slip, and the agreed-upon position that repentance guarantees forgiveness.

Core Idea
Repentance guarantees forgiveness; the real debate concerns unrepented sins.

Many theologians debate whether Allah forgives without repentance, but there is consensus on one point: repentance removes punishment. This lesson examines a famous critique of al-Taftazani, who mistakenly treated the agreed-upon case as controversial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's argument against al-Taftazani's slip.
The passage is dense; focus on the two reasons for the slip.
2
Identify the two contradictions
The author says al-Taftazani's statement is a slip for two respects. Find these two respects and note how they relate to the Mu'tazila and the consensus.
One respect involves the phrase 'with repentance' itself.
3
Understand the consensus on repentance
The passage asserts that all groups agree on forgiveness after repentance, citing a hadith and a Quranic verse. Reflect on how this undermines al-Taftazani's position.
This consensus is key to the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول التفتازاني رحمه الله في "شرح العقائد" عند قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ [النساء:٤٨] من الصغائر والكبائر مع التوبة أو بدونها خلافا للمعتزلة، ففيه أن قوله مع التوبة سهو قلم ليس في محله من جهتين حيث خالف الطائفتين؛ لأن المشيئة بدون التوبة محل خلاف للمعتزلة، وأما معها فلا خلاف في المسألة كما صرح في "شرح المقاصد"، بأنهم أجمعوا على أن لا عذاب على التائب كما صح في الحديث : التائب من الذنب كمن لا ذنب له)) . () وكقوله تعالى: وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ ﴾ [الشورى: ٢٥].
As for the saying of al-Taftazani (may Allah have mercy on him) in Sharh al-'Aqa'id regarding His statement, the Exalted: {And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills} [al-Nisa' 4:48] from minor and major sins with repentance or without it, contrary to the Mu'tazila, then it contains [the objection] that his statement 'with repentance' is a slip of the pen, not in its place, from two respects: he contradicted both groups; because the will [to forgive] without repentance is a point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila, but with repentance there is no disagreement in the matter, as he clarified in Sharh al-Maqasid, that they have agreed that there is no punishment for the one who repents, as established in the hadith: 'The one who repents from sin is like one who has no sin,' and similarly His statement, the Exalted: {And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants} [al-Shura 42:25].
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific statement made by al-Taftazani in his commentary on the creed. The author points out that al-Taftazani erred by saying that Allah's forgiveness of sins can be with or without repentance, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The critique argues that this is a slip (sahw qalam) for two reasons. First, al-Taftazani's statement that forgiveness without repentance is contested by the Mu'tazila is correct, but then he confused matters regarding forgiveness with repentance. Second, the true position is that forgiveness with repentance is not disputed at all; all groups agree that a repentant person will not be punished, as supported by the hadith 'the repentant sinner is like one without sin' and the Quranic verse that Allah accepts repentance. Thus, the mistake is in saying 'with repentance' as if it were a point of contention.

Key Terms
توبة (repentance)
مغفرة (forgiveness)
إجماع (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the debate is about whether God ever forgives without repentance.
Correction: The actual disagreement is about forgiveness without repentance, not with it. Repentance is agreed to guarantee forgiveness.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with al-Taftazani's statement about forgiveness?
A) It contradicts the Mu'tazila completely.
B) It wrongly includes the Mu'tazila's position as valid.
C) It is a slip because there is no disagreement on forgiveness with repentance.
D) It ignores the hadith on repentance.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's error was treating forgiveness after repentance as a point of disagreement when it is actually a matter of consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If repentance guarantees forgiveness, what sin are you holding onto that you haven't brought to Allah? What is stopping you from turning back to Him right now?
?
The Mu'tazila agreed that God forgives the repentant, yet they argued about the fate of a Muslim who dies without repenting. Which category do you fear you might belong to, and what does this lesson compel you to do about it?
?
You have read that there is consensus on forgiveness after repentance. How does that change the way you view your own past sins? Will you actually repent today, or will you continue to let false shame keep you from Allah's mercy?

The Purpose of Evil: Manifestations of Majesty and Beauty
The commentator explains that both good and evil figures manifest God's attributes of majesty and beauty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both good and evil figures serve as manifestations of God's attributes of majesty and beauty, and that evil has a purpose in the divine plan.

Core Idea
Evil is not meaningless; it manifests God's majesty and is necessary for perfection.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar explores the theological wisdom behind the existence of evil figures like Iblis and Pharaoh. It argues that they are not mere aberrations but reflect the divine attributes of jalal (majesty) and jamal (beauty), both required for the full display of divine perfection. Let us see how the commentator develops this profound insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the key terms: جلال, جمال, ظهورات.
The passage is dense; read it once without analyzing.
2
Identify the two manifestations
The commentator states that the first (Iblis) is from manifestations of majesty, and the second (Pharaoh) from manifestations of beauty. Confirm who 'the first' and 'the second' refer to.
From context, 'the first' refers to Iblis, 'the second' to Pharaoh.
3
Understand the necessity
The passage says both are necessary 'for the appearance of the light of the attribute of perfection'. Reflect on why both manifestations are needed.
4
Explore the quote from Abu Madyan
Sheikh Abu Madyan says: 'Do not deny falsehood in its state, for it is one of His manifestations.' Think about what this means for the existence of evil.
This is a key sufi insight.
5
Examine the hadith about Iblis and Pharaoh
The passage ends with a hadith where Gabriel says he hates Iblis and Pharaoh most. Notice that the commentator cites this to show that even prophets acknowledge the reality of evil figures.
Imam Abu Hanifa combined them in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأول من مظاهر الجلال، والثاني من مظاهر الجمال ولابد منهما لظهور نور نعت الكمال، ولذا قال الشيخ أبو مدين المغربي رضي الله عنه: لا تنكر الباطل في طوره فإنه بعض ظهوراته يعني باعتبار تجليات صفاته في مرأى مصنوعاته، وإنما جمع الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله بين إبليس وفرعون رضي عنه بلغنا أن جبرائيل عليه السلام قال لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما أبغضت عبدا من عباد الله ما أبغضت عبدين: أحدهما من الجن، والآخر من الإنس، وأما الذي من الجن فإبليس حين أبى أن يسجد لآدم عليه السلام، وأما الذي من الإنس ففرعون ذي التلبيس لما روي عن السدي
The first is from manifestations of majesty (جلال), and the second from manifestations of beauty (جمال), and they are both necessary for the appearance of the light of the attribute of perfection. For this reason, Sheikh Abu Madyan al-Maghribi, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Do not deny falsehood in its state, for it is one of His manifestations' — meaning in consideration of the self-disclosures (تجلّيات) of His attributes in the mirror of His creatures. And indeed, Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, combined between Iblis and Pharaoh — it has reached us that Gabriel, peace be upon him, said to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, 'I have not hated any of the servants of Allah as I have hated two servants: one from the jinn and the other from mankind. As for the one from the jinn, Iblis, when he refused to prostrate to Adam, peace be upon him; and as for the one from mankind, Pharaoh, the one of feigning, due to what is narrated from al-Suddi.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a theological reflection on the purpose of evil. The commentator states that Iblis (from the jinn) represents manifestations of divine majesty (jalal), while the Prophet (from mankind) represents manifestations of beauty (jamal). Both are necessary for the appearance of perfection. He quotes Sheikh Abu Madyan al-Maghribi: 'Do not deny falsehood in its state, for it is one of His manifestations' — meaning they are self-disclosures of God's attributes. He then mentions that Imam Abu Hanifa combined Iblis and Pharaoh in a hadith where Gabriel said he hated two servants most: Iblis and Pharaoh, the one of feigning (talbis). This shows the wisdom in the existence of both.

Key Terms
جلال
جمال
إبليس
فرعون
ظهورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil exists because God cannot prevent it or because it is not part of His plan.
Correction: The passage teaches that evil figures like Iblis and Pharaoh are manifestations of God's attributes (jalal and jamal) and are necessary for the display of divine perfection. Their existence has a purpose in the divine plan.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why are both Iblis and Pharaoh necessary?
A) Because God loves evil
B) For the manifestation of divine perfection through majesty and beauty
C) Because they are equally good
D) To test humans

Key Takeaway
Both good and evil are necessary manifestations of God's attributes of majesty and beauty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter people you consider evil—like a tyrant or a betrayer—do you see them as merely obstacles, or can you glimpse God's majestic attribute of power through them? What would it take for you to look for His manifestation in them?
?
This lesson claims that even falsehood is 'one of His manifestations.' Is there a falsehood in your own life—a sin, a trial, or a loss—that you have resented as meaningless? Will you now ask Allah to show you how that very thing reveals His perfection?
?
Imam Abu Hanifa combined Iblis and Pharaoh in one category. Which person in your life do you most despise? Can you make the intention (niyyah) to see that person not as pure evil, but as a vessel through which Allah manifests His jalal or jamal, and change your attitude toward them starting today?
